     <p><b>The University of Texas at Austin</b> (<b>University of Texas</b>, <b>UT Austin</b>, <b>UT</b>, or simply <b>Texas</b>) is a public research-intensive university located in Austin, Texas, United States. It is the flagship institution of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_System" title="University of Texas System">University of Texas&nbsp;System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-flagship1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flagship1-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-flagship4_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flagship4-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-flagship5_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flagship5-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Founded in 1883, its campus is approximately 0.25 miles (400&nbsp;m) from the Texas State Capitol in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a>. The institution has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_United_States_university_campuses_by_enrollment" title="List of largest United States university campuses by enrollment" class="mw-redirect">fifth-largest</a> single-campus enrollment in the nation, with over 50,000 undergraduate and graduate students and over 24,000 faculty and staff.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University of Texas at Austin was named one of the original eight <a href="/wiki/Public_Ivy" title="Public Ivy">Public Ivy</a> institutions<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> and was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_of_Universities" title="American Association of Universities" class="mw-redirect">American Association of Universities</a> in 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is a major center for academic research, with research expenditures exceeding $640 million for the 2009?2010 school year.<sup id="cite_ref-utexas.edu_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utexas.edu-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> The university houses seven museums and seventeen libraries, including the <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Baines_Johnson_Library_and_Museum" title="Lyndon Baines Johnson Library and Museum">Lyndon Baines Johnson Library and Museum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Blanton_Museum_of_Art" title="Blanton Museum of Art">Blanton Museum of Art</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> and operates various auxiliary research facilities, such as the <a href="/wiki/J._J._Pickle_Research_Campus" title="J. J. Pickle Research Campus">J. J. Pickle Research Campus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/McDonald_Observatory" title="McDonald Observatory">McDonald Observatory</a>. Among university faculty are recipients of the Nobel Prize, <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Wolf_Prize" title="Wolf Prize">Wolf Prize</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a>, as well as many other awards.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>UT Austin student athletes compete as the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Longhorns" title="Texas Longhorns">Texas Longhorns</a> and are members of the <a href="/wiki/Big_12_Conference" title="Big 12 Conference">Big 12 Conference</a>. The university has won four <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I" title="NCAA Division I" class="mw-redirect">NCAA Division I</a> National Football Championships, and has claimed more titles in men's and women's sports than any other school in the Big 12 since the league was founded in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-UT_Athletics_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UT_Athletics-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Current and former UT Austin athletes have won 117 Olympic medals, including 14 in Beijing in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> The university was recognized by <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i> as &quot;America's Best Sports College&quot; in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

          <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Student_life"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Student life</span></a> 
           <ul> 
            <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Student_profile"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Student profile</span></a></li> 
            <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Residential_life"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Residential life</span></a></li>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="History of the University of Texas at Austin">History of the University of Texas at Austin</a>
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment">Establishment</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Establishment">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The first mention of a public university in Texas can be traced to the 1827 constitution for the <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexican</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Coahuila_y_Tejas" title="Coahuila y Tejas">Coahuila y Tejas</a>. Although an article promised to establish public education in the arts and sciences, no action was taken by the Mexican government. But after Texas obtained its <a href="/wiki/Texas_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Texas Declaration of Independence">independence</a> from Mexico in 1836, the Congress of Texas adopted the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Republic_of_Texas" title="Constitution of the Republic of Texas">Constitution of the Republic</a>, which included a provision to establish public education in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Texas" title="Republic of Texas">republic</a>, including two universities or colleges. On January 26, 1839, the Congress of Texas agreed to eventually set aside fifty <a href="/wiki/League_(unit)" title="League (unit)">leagues</a> of land towards the effort; in addition, 40 acres (160,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) in the new capital of Austin were reserved and designated &quot;College Hill.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-TSHA_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TSHA-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> (The term &quot;Forty Acres&quot; is colloquially used to refer to the University as a whole.)</p> 
     <p>In 1845, Texas was <a href="/wiki/Texas_Annexation" title="Texas Annexation" class="mw-redirect">annexed</a> into the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> of America. The state legislature passed the Act of 1858, which set aside $100,000 in United States <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">bonds</a> towards construction of a university. In addition, the legislature designated land, previously reserved for the encouragement of railroad construction, toward the universities' fifty leagues. But Texas's <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secession</a> from the Union and the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> prevented further action on these plans.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Oldmainbuilding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/Oldmainbuilding.jpg/220px-Oldmainbuilding.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/Oldmainbuilding.jpg/330px-Oldmainbuilding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/Oldmainbuilding.jpg/440px-Oldmainbuilding.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Oldmainbuilding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The university's Old Main Building in 1903
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>After the war, the 1862 <a href="/wiki/Morrill_Land-Grant_Colleges_Act" title="Morrill Land-Grant Colleges Act" class="mw-redirect">Morrill Act</a> facilitated the creation of what is now <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University" title="Texas A&amp;M University">Texas A&amp;M University</a>, which was established in 1876 as the Agricultural &amp; Mechanical College of Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-TSHA_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TSHA-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Texas_Constitution" title="Texas Constitution" class="mw-redirect">Texas Constitution of 1876</a> mandated that the state establish a university &quot;at an early day,&quot; calling for the creation of a &quot;university of the first class,&quot; styled &quot;<b>The University of Texas.</b>&quot; It revoked the endowment of the railroad lands of the Act of 1858 but appropriated 1,000,000 acres (4,000&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) in West Texas. In 1883, another two million were granted, with income from the sale of land and <a href="/wiki/Grazing_rights" title="Grazing rights">grazing rights</a> going to The University of Texas and Texas A&amp;M.<sup id="cite_ref-TSHA_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TSHA-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1881, Austin was chosen as the site of the main university, and Galveston was designated the location of the medical department. On the original &quot;College Hill,&quot; an official ceremony began construction on what is now referred to as the old Main Building in late 1882. The university opened its doors on September 15, 1883.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_and_growth">Expansion and growth</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Expansion and growth">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UT_tower_lit_entirely_in_orange.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/UT_tower_lit_entirely_in_orange.jpg/170px-UT_tower_lit_entirely_in_orange.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/UT_tower_lit_entirely_in_orange.jpg/255px-UT_tower_lit_entirely_in_orange.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/UT_tower_lit_entirely_in_orange.jpg/340px-UT_tower_lit_entirely_in_orange.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UT_tower_lit_entirely_in_orange.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Main_Building_(University_of_Texas_at_Austin)" title="Main Building (University of Texas at Austin)">The Tower</a>, completed in 1937, stands 307 ft (94 m) tall and dons 
        <a href="/wiki/Main_Building_(University_of_Texas_at_Austin)#Lighting" title="Main Building (University of Texas at Austin)">different colors of lighting on special occasions.</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The old <a href="/wiki/Victorian_architecture" title="Victorian architecture">Victorian</a>-<a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic</a> <a href="/wiki/Main_Building_(University_of_Texas_at_Austin)" title="Main Building (University of Texas at Austin)">Main Building</a> served as the central point of the campus's 40-acre (160,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) site, and was used for nearly all purposes. But by the 1930s, discussions arose about the need for new library space, and the Main Building was razed in 1934 over the objections of many students and faculty. The modern-day tower and Main Building were constructed in its place.</p> 
     <p>In 1910, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Brackenridge" title="George Washington Brackenridge">George Washington Brackenridge</a> donated 500 acres (2.0&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) on the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_River_(Texas)" title="Colorado River (Texas)">Colorado River</a> to the university . A vote by the regents to move the campus to the donated land was met with outrage, and the land has only been used for auxiliary purposes such as graduate student housing. Part of the tract was sold in the late-1990s for luxury housing, and there are controversial proposals to sell the remainder of the tract. The <a href="/wiki/Brackenridge_Field_Laboratory" title="Brackenridge Field Laboratory">Brackenridge Field Laboratory</a> was established on 82 acres (330,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) of the land in 1967.</p> 
     <p>As a result of the controversy, in 1921, the legislature appropriated $1,350,000 for the purchase of land adjacent to the main campus. But expansion was hampered by the constitutional restriction against funding the construction of buildings. With the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> on university-owned grounds in 1923, the institution was able to put its new wealth towards its general endowment fund. These savings allowed the passing of amendments to make way for bond issues in 1931 and 1947, with the latter expansion necessary from the spike in enrollment following World War II. The university built 19 permanent structures between 1950 and 1965, when it was given the right of <a href="/wiki/Eminent_domain" title="Eminent domain">eminent domain</a>. With this power, the university purchased additional properties surrounding the original 40 acres (160,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>).</p> 
     <p>During World War II, the University of Texas was one of 131 colleges and universities nationally that took part in the <a href="/wiki/V-12_Navy_College_Training_Program" title="V-12 Navy College Training Program">V-12 Navy College Training Program</a> which offered students a path to a Navy commission.<sup id="cite_ref-list-of-v-12_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-list-of-v-12-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1966_shooting_spree">1966 shooting spree</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1966 shooting spree">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>On August 1, 1966, Texas student <a href="/wiki/Charles_Whitman" title="Charles Whitman">Charles Whitman</a> barricaded the observation deck in the tower of the Main Building. With two rifles, a <a href="/wiki/Sawed-off_shotgun" title="Sawed-off shotgun">sawed-off shotgun</a> and various other weapons, he killed 16 people on campus from the observation deck, below the clocks on the tower and three more in the tower, as well as wounding two more inside the observation deck. Whitman had been a patient at the University Health Center, and on March 29, preceding the shootings, had conveyed to psychiatrist Maurice Heatley his feelings of overwhelming hostilities and that he was thinking about &quot;going up on the tower with a deer rifle and start shooting people.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the Whitman event, the observation deck was closed until 1968, and then closed again in 1975 following a series of suicide jumps during the 1970s. In 1999, after installation of security fencing and other safety precautions, the tower observation deck reopened to the public.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Recent history">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The first <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">presidential library</a> on a university campus was dedicated on May 22, 1971 with former <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">President Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lady_Bird_Johnson" title="Lady Bird Johnson">Lady Bird Johnson</a> and then-President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> in attendance. Constructed on the eastern side of the main campus, the <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Baines_Johnson_Library_and_Museum" title="Lyndon Baines Johnson Library and Museum">Lyndon Baines Johnson Library and Museum</a> is one of 13 presidential libraries administered by the <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">National Archives and Records Administration</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:DKR_2008_longhorns.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/DKR_2008_longhorns.jpg/220px-DKR_2008_longhorns.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/DKR_2008_longhorns.jpg/330px-DKR_2008_longhorns.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/DKR_2008_longhorns.jpg/440px-DKR_2008_longhorns.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:DKR_2008_longhorns.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Darrell_K._Royal-Texas_Memorial_Stadium" title="Darrell K. Royal-Texas Memorial Stadium" class="mw-redirect">Darrell K. Royal-Texas Memorial Stadium</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The University of Texas has experienced a wave of new construction recently with several significant buildings. On April 30, 2006, the school opened the <a href="/wiki/Blanton_Museum_of_Art" title="Blanton Museum of Art">Blanton Museum of Art</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> In August 2008, the AT&amp;T Executive Education and Conference Center opened, with the hotel and conference center forming part of a new gateway to the university. Also in 2008, <a href="/wiki/Darrell_K_Royal-Texas_Memorial_Stadium" title="Darrell K Royal-Texas Memorial Stadium" class="mw-redirect">Darrell K Royal-Texas Memorial Stadium</a> was expanded to a seating capacity of 100,119, making it the largest stadium (by capacity) in the state of Texas at the time, now surpassed by Cowboy Stadium.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>On the morning of September 28, 2010, 19-year-old Colton Tooley opened fire on campus with an <a href="/wiki/AK-47" title="AK-47">AK-47</a> <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_rifle" title="Semi-automatic rifle">semi-automatic rifle</a>, resulting in a lockdown of the university campus. He then walked into the Perry-Casta&ntilde;eda Library and committed suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>On January 19, 2011, the university announced the creation of a 24-hour television network in partnership with <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>, dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Longhorn_Network" title="Longhorn Network">Longhorn Network</a>. ESPN will pay a $300 million guaranteed rights fee over 20 years to the university and to IMG College, UT Austin's multimedia rights partner. The network covers the university's intercollegiate athletics, music, cultural arts and academics programs. The channel first aired in September 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_University_of_Texas_at_Austin_buildings" title="List of University of Texas at Austin buildings">List of University of Texas at Austin buildings</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The University's property totals 1,438.5 acres (582.14&nbsp;ha), comprising the 423.5 acres (171.38&nbsp;ha) for the Main Campus in central <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a> and other the <a href="/wiki/J._J._Pickle_Research_Campus" title="J. J. Pickle Research Campus">J. J. Pickle Research Campus</a> in north Austin and the other properties throughout Texas. The main campus has 150 buildings totalling over 18,000,000 square feet (1,700,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>).</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Johnson_library.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Johnson_library.jpg/220px-Johnson_library.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/Johnson_library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/Johnson_library.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Johnson_library.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Baines_Johnson_Library_and_Museum" title="Lyndon Baines Johnson Library and Museum">Lyndon Baines Johnson Library and Museum</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Gregory_Gym.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Gregory_Gym.jpg/220px-Gregory_Gym.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Gregory_Gym.jpg/330px-Gregory_Gym.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Gregory_Gym.jpg/440px-Gregory_Gym.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Gregory_Gym.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Gymnasium" title="Gregory Gymnasium">Gregory Gymnasium</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>One of the University's most visible features is the <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts</a> <a href="/wiki/Main_Building_(University_of_Texas_at_Austin)" title="Main Building (University of Texas at Austin)">Main Building</a>, including a 307-foot (94&nbsp;m) tower designed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Philippe_Cret" title="Paul Philippe Cret">Paul Philippe Cret</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Completed in 1937, the Main Building is in the middle of campus. The tower usually appears illuminated in white light in the evening but is lit orange for various special occasions, including athletic victories and academic accomplishments; it is conversely darkened for solemn occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> At the top of the tower is a <a href="/wiki/Carillon" title="Carillon">carillon</a> of 56 bells, the largest in Texas. Songs are played on weekdays by <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.texascarillon.com">student carillonneurs</a>, in addition to the usual pealing of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Quarters" title="Westminster Quarters">Westminster Quarters</a> every quarter hour between 6&nbsp;am and 9&nbsp;pm<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1998, after the installation of security and safety measures, the observation deck reopened to the public indefinitely for weekend tours.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Littlefield_house_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Littlefield_house_2007.jpg/220px-Littlefield_house_2007.jpg" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Littlefield_house_2007.jpg/330px-Littlefield_house_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Littlefield_house_2007.jpg/440px-Littlefield_house_2007.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Littlefield_house_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Littlefield_House" title="Littlefield House">Littlefield House</a>, used today by the university's Office of Resource Development, was constructed in 1893 and is listed in the 
        <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The university's seven museums and seventeen libraries hold over nine million volumes, making it the seventh-largest academic library in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> The holdings of the university's <a href="/wiki/Harry_Ransom_Humanities_Research_Center" title="Harry Ransom Humanities Research Center" class="mw-redirect">Harry Ransom Humanities Research Center</a> include one of only 21 remaining complete copies of the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a> and the first permanent photograph, <i><a href="/wiki/View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras" title="View from the Window at Le Gras">View from the Window at Le Gras</a></i>, taken by <a href="/wiki/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce" title="Nic&eacute;phore Ni&eacute;pce">Nic&eacute;phore Ni&eacute;pce</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The newest museum, the 155,000-square-foot (14,400&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Blanton_Museum_of_Art" title="Blanton Museum of Art">Blanton Museum of Art</a>, is the largest university art museum in the United States and hosts approximately 17,000 works from Europe, the United States, and Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University of Texas has an extensive underground tunnel system that links all of the buildings on campus. Constructed in the 1930s under the supervision of creator Carl Eckhardt, then head of the physical plant, the tunnels have grown along with the university campus. They currently measure approximately six miles in total length.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> The tunnel system is used for communications and utility service. It is closed to the public and is guarded by <a href="/wiki/Burglar_alarm" title="Burglar alarm">silent alarms</a>. Since the late 1940s the university has generated its own electricity. Today its natural gas cogeneration plant has a capacity of 123 MW. The university also operates a <a href="/wiki/TRIGA" title="TRIGA">TRIGA</a> nuclear reactor at the J.J. Pickle Research Campus.<sup id="cite_ref-Nuclear_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nuclear_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university continues to expand its facilities on campus. In 2010, the university opened the state-of-the-art Norman Hackerman building (on the location of the former Experimental Sciences Building) housing chemistry and biology research and teaching laboratories. In 2010, the university broke ground on the $120 million Bill &amp; Melinda Gates Computer Science Complex and Dell Computer Science Hall and the $51 million Belo Center for New Media, both of which are now complete.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The new <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">LEED</a> gold-certified, 110,000-square-foot (10,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) Student Activity Center (SAC) opened in January 2011, housing study rooms, lounges and food vendors. The SAC was constructed as a result of a student referendum passed in 2006 which raised student fees by $65 per semester.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university operates a public radio station, <a href="/wiki/KUT" title="KUT">KUT</a>, which provides local <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcasting" title="FM broadcasting">FM broadcasts</a> as well as live streaming audio over the Internet. The university uses <a href="/wiki/Capital_Metropolitan_Transportation_Authority" title="Capital Metropolitan Transportation Authority">Capital Metro</a> to provide bus transportation for students around the campus and throughout Austin.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_profile">Academic profile</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Academic profile">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

     <div class="thumb tright">

       <a href="/wiki/File:UT_Austin_Mustangs_campus.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/UT_Austin_Mustangs_campus.JPG/220px-UT_Austin_Mustangs_campus.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/UT_Austin_Mustangs_campus.JPG/330px-UT_Austin_Mustangs_campus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/UT_Austin_Mustangs_campus.JPG/440px-UT_Austin_Mustangs_campus.JPG 2x" /></a>

        <a href="/wiki/Cockrell_School_of_Engineering" title="Cockrell School of Engineering">Engineering Sciences buildings</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>UT Austin is consistently ranked as one of the top public universities in the country, with highly prestigious programs in a variety of fields. Nationally, UT Austin ranked 45th amongst all universities according to <i>U.S. News and World Report</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> and tied for 13th place among public universities in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_School_of_Architecture" title="University of Texas School of Architecture">University of Texas School of Architecture</a> was ranked second among national undergraduate programs in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/McCombs_School_of_Business" title="McCombs School of Business">McCombs School of Business</a> was ranked seventh among undergraduate business programs in 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Cockrell_School_of_Engineering" title="Cockrell School of Engineering">Cockrell School of Engineering</a> was ranked ninth among undergraduate engineering programs in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Internationally, UT Austin was ranked 67th in the &quot;World's Best Universities&quot; ranking presented by <i>U.S. News and World Report</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> and 35th in the world by <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Jiao_Tong_University" title="Shanghai Jiao Tong University">Shanghai Jiao Tong University</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ARWU_2009_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARWU_2009-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> based on factors such as Nobel laureate affiliation and number of highly cited researchers. In 2009, <a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a> ranked the school 49th worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2013 London-based<a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education" title="Times Higher Education">Times Higher Education</a> ranked the university 25th in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Human_Resources_%26_Labor_Review" title="Human Resources &amp; Labor Review" class="mw-redirect">Human Resources &amp; Labor Review</a> ranked the university 42th and QS' &quot;World University Rankings&quot;ranked the university 67th internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><br /></p> 
     <p>As of 2013, <i>U.S. News and World Report</i> ranked the Accounting and Latin American History programs as the top in the nation. Additionally, more than 50 other science, humanities and professional programs rank in the top 25 nationally, according to U.S. News &amp; World Report's latest edition of ?Best Graduate Schools.?<sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The College of Education and College of Pharmacy are each the fourth best in the nation in their fields (with Education ranking first among public universities for the third year in a row and also number one in research expeditures). And the School of Information (iSchool) is sixth best in Library and Information Sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Among other overall school rankings, the <a href="/wiki/Cockrell_School_of_Engineering" title="Cockrell School of Engineering">Cockrell School of Engineering</a> is 11th best (sixth among publics). The <a href="/wiki/McCombs_School_of_Business" title="McCombs School of Business">McCombs School of Business</a> is 17th best (fifth among publics). The <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_School_of_Public_Affairs" title="Lyndon B. Johnson School of Public Affairs">Lyndon B. Johnson School of Public Affairs</a> remains at No. 16, the <a href="/wiki/Jackson_School_of_Geosciences" title="Jackson School of Geosciences">Jackson School of Geosciences</a> remains at No. 9 for Earth Sciences, and the School of Social Work remains at No. 7.<sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_School_of_Law" title="University of Texas School of Law">University of Texas School of Law</a> climbed one place in the rankings, to No. 15 in the nation (fourth among publics).<sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>A 2005 <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg</a> survey ranked the school 5th among all business schools and first among public business schools for the largest number of alumni who are <a href="/wiki/S%26P_500" title="S&amp;P 500">S&amp;P 500</a> CEOs.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Similarly, a 2005 <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i> report ranked the university as &quot;the number one source of new <a href="/wiki/Fortune_1000" title="Fortune 1000">Fortune 1000</a> CEOs.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>A &quot;payback&quot; analysis published by SmartMoney in 2011 comparing graduates' salaries to tuition costs concluded that the school was the second-best value of all colleges in the nation, behind only <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Tech" title="Georgia Tech" class="mw-redirect">Georgia Tech</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> A 2013 College Database study found that UT was 22nd in the nation in terms of increased lifetime earnings by graduates.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colleges_and_schools">Colleges and schools</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Colleges and schools">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The university contains eighteen colleges &amp; schools and one academic unit, each listed with its founding date:<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table style="border:1px solid #ddd; background:#fefefe; padding:3px; margin:0;">

     </table> 
     <p>In addition, the university has seven honors programs that span a variety of academic fields: Liberal Arts Honors, the Business Honors Program, the Turing Scholars Program in Computer Sciences, Engineering Honors, the Health Science Honors Program in Natural Sciences, the Dean's Scholars Honors Program in Natural Sciences, and the interdisciplinary Plan II Honors program. The university also offers innovative programs for promoting academic excellence and leadership development such as the <a href="/wiki/Freshman_Research_Initiative" title="Freshman Research Initiative">Freshman Research Initiative</a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.utexas.edu/tip/">Texas Interdisciplinary Plan</a>.</p> 
     <p>The University of Texas at Austin offers more than 100 undergraduate and 170 graduate degrees. In the 2009?2010 academic year, the university awarded a total of 13,215 degrees: 67.7% <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor's degree">bachelor's degrees</a>, 22.0% master's degrees, 6.4% <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctoral degrees</a>, and 3.9% Professional degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Admission">Admission</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Admission">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Nhb_utexas.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Nhb_utexas.jpg/220px-Nhb_utexas.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Nhb_utexas.jpg/330px-Nhb_utexas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Nhb_utexas.jpg/440px-Nhb_utexas.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Nhb_utexas.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Norman Hackerman Building
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>As a state public university, The University of Texas at Austin was, until recently, subject to <a href="/wiki/Texas_House_Bill_588" title="Texas House Bill 588">Texas House Bill 588</a>, which guarantees graduating Texas high school seniors in the top 10% of their class admission to any public Texas university. A new state law granting UT (but no other state university) a partial exemption from the top 10% rule, Senate Bill 175, was passed by the 81st Legislature in 2009. It modifies this admissions policy by limiting automatically admitted freshmen to 75% of the entering in-state freshman class, starting in 2011. The university will admit the top one percent, the top two percent and so forth until the cap is reached; the university expects to automatically admit students in the top 8% of their graduating class for 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Furthermore, students admitted under <a href="/wiki/Texas_House_Bill_588" title="Texas House Bill 588">Texas House Bill 588</a> are not guaranteed their choice of college or major, but rather only guaranteed admission to the university as a whole. Many colleges, such as the Cockrell School of Engineering, have secondary requirements that must be met for admission.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>For others who go through the traditional application process, selectivity is deemed &quot;more selective&quot; according to the Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> For Fall 2009, 31,362 applied and 45.6% were accepted, and of those accepted, 51.0% enrolled.<sup id="cite_ref-2009-2010_Common_Data_Set_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2009-2010_Common_Data_Set-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> The university's freshman retention rate in 2009 was 92.5% and the six-year graduation rate was 81.0%.<sup id="cite_ref-2009-2010_Common_Data_Set_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2009-2010_Common_Data_Set-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> The Fall 2011 entering class had an average ACT composite score of 28 and an average SAT composite score of 1858.<sup id="cite_ref-12th_Class_Day_Fall_2011_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12th_Class_Day_Fall_2011-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faculty_and_research">Faculty and research</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Faculty and research">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:USA_harlan_j_smith_telescope_TX.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/USA_harlan_j_smith_telescope_TX.jpg/220px-USA_harlan_j_smith_telescope_TX.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/USA_harlan_j_smith_telescope_TX.jpg/330px-USA_harlan_j_smith_telescope_TX.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/USA_harlan_j_smith_telescope_TX.jpg/440px-USA_harlan_j_smith_telescope_TX.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:USA_harlan_j_smith_telescope_TX.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Harlan J. Smith Telescope
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div>

      <a href="/wiki/List_of_University_of_Texas_at_Austin_faculty" title="List of University of Texas at Austin faculty">List of University of Texas at Austin faculty</a>
     </div> 
     <p>In Fall 2009, the school employed 2,770 <a href="/wiki/Full-time_equivalent" title="Full-time equivalent">full-time</a> faculty members (88.3% of whom hold the terminal degree in their field), with a student-to-faculty ratio of 17.3 to 1.<sup id="cite_ref-2009-2010_Common_Data_Set_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2009-2010_Common_Data_Set-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> The university's faculty includes 63 members of the National Academy,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> winners of the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Technology" title="National Medal of Technology" class="mw-redirect">National Medal of Technology</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Turing_Award" title="Turing Award">Turing Award</a> and other various awards.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Nine Nobel Laureates are or have been affiliated with UT Austin.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Except for <a href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT" class="mw-redirect">MIT</a>, UT Austin attracts more federal research grants than any American university without a medical school.<sup id="cite_ref-utexas.edu_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utexas.edu-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> For the 2009?2010 school year, the university exceeded $640 million in research funding (up from $590 million the previous year)<sup id="cite_ref-utexas.edu_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utexas.edu-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> and has earned more than 300 patents since 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-utexas1_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utexas1-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> UT Austin houses the Office of Technology Commercialization, a <a href="/wiki/Technology_transfer" title="Technology transfer">technology transfer</a> center which serves as the bridge between laboratory research and commercial development. In 2009, UT Austin created nine new start-up companies to commercialize technology developed at the university and has created 46 start-ups in the past seven years. UT Austin license agreements generated $10.9 million in revenue for the university in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-utexas1_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utexas1-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Research at UT Austin is largely focused in the engineering and physical sciences,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> and is a world-leading research institution in fields such as computer science.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Energy is a major research thrust of the university, with major federally funded projects on biofuels,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> battery and solar cell technology, and geological carbon dioxide storage,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> water purification membranes, among others. In 2009, UT founded the Energy Institute, led by former Under Secretary for Science <a href="/wiki/Raymond_L._Orbach" title="Raymond L. Orbach">Raymond L. Orbach</a>, to organize and advance multi-disciplinary energy research at the university.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> While the university does not have a medical school, it houses medical programs associated with other campuses and allied health professional programs, as well as major research programs in pharmacy, biomedical engineering, and neuroscience and others. UT Austin opened the $100 million Dell Pediatric Research Institute in 2010 as part of an effort to increase medical research at the university and establish a medical research complex, and associated medical school, in the city of Austin.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>UT Austin operates several major auxiliary research centers. The world's third-largest telescope, the <a href="/wiki/Hobby-Eberly_Telescope" title="Hobby-Eberly Telescope">Hobby-Eberly Telescope</a>, and three other large telescopes are part of UT's <a href="/wiki/McDonald_Observatory" title="McDonald Observatory">McDonald Observatory</a>, 450 miles (720&nbsp;km) west of Austin.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> The university manages nearly 300 acres (1.2&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) of biological field laboratories, including the <a href="/wiki/Brackenridge_Field_Laboratory" title="Brackenridge Field Laboratory">Brackenridge Field Laboratory</a> in Austin. The <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Agile_Technology" title="Center for Agile Technology">Center for Agile Technology</a> focuses on <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">software development</a> challenges.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/J.J._Pickle_Research_Campus" title="J.J. Pickle Research Campus" class="mw-redirect">J.J. Pickle Research Campus</a> (PRC) is home to the Texas Advanced Computing Center which operates the Ranger supercomputer, one of the most powerful supercomputers in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as the Microelectronics Research Center which houses micro- and nanoelectronics research and features a 15,000 square foot (1,400&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) cleanroom for device fabrication. Founded in 1946, UT's Applied Research Laboratories at the PRC has been responsible for the development or testing of the vast majority of high-frequency sonar equipment used by the Navy, and in 2007, was granted a research contract by the Navy funded up to $928 million over ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Transportation_Research_UT_Austin" title="Center for Transportation Research UT Austin">Center for Transportation Research UT Austin</a> is a nationally recognized research institution focusing on transportation research, education, and public service. Established in 1963 as the Center for Highway Research, its current and ongoing projects address virtually all aspects of transportation, including economics, multimodal systems, traffic congestion relief, transportation policy, materials, structures, transit, environmental impacts, driver behavior, land use, geometric design, accessibility, and pavements.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Endowment">Endowment</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Endowment">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Permanent_University_Fund" title="Permanent University Fund">Permanent University Fund</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The university has an endowment of $7.2 billion, out of the $16.11 billion (according to 2008 estimates) available to the University of Texas System. This figure reflects the fact that the school has the largest endowment of any public university in the nation.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Thirty percent of the university's endowment comes from <a href="/wiki/Permanent_University_Fund" title="Permanent University Fund">Permanent University Fund</a> (PUF), with nearly $15 billion in assets as of 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> Proceeds from lands appropriated in 1839 and 1876, as well as oil monies, comprise the majority of PUF. At one time, the PUF was the chief source of income for Texas' two university systems, The University of Texas System and the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_System" title="Texas A&amp;M University System">Texas A&amp;M University System</a>; today, however, its revenues account for less than 10 percent of the universities' annual budgets. This has challenged the universities to increase sponsored research and private donations. Privately funded endowments contribute over $2 billion to the University's total endowment value.</p> 
     <p>The university is one of only two public universities in the U.S. that have a triple-A credit rating from all three major credit rating agencies, along with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_life">Student life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Student life">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_profile">Student profile</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Student profile">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>For Fall 2011, the university enrolled 38,437 undergraduate, 11,497 graduate and 1,178 law students.<sup id="cite_ref-Enrollment_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enrollment-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Out-of-state and international students comprised 9.1% of the undergraduate student body and 20.1% of the total student body, with students from all 50 states and more than 120 foreign countries?most notably, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, followed by the People's Republic of China, India, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Student_Characteristics_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Student_Characteristics-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> For Fall 2010, the undergraduate student body was 48.7% male and 51.3% female.<sup id="cite_ref-Enrollment_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enrollment-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> The three largest undergraduate majors in 2009 were Biological Sciences, Unspecified Business, and Psychology, while the three largest graduate majors were Business Administration (<a href="/wiki/MBA" title="MBA" class="mw-redirect">MBA</a>), Electrical and Computer Engineering, and Pharmacy (<a href="/wiki/PharmD" title="PharmD" class="mw-redirect">PharmD</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Student_Characteristics_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Student_Characteristics-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Residential_life">Residential life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Residential life">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The campus is currently home to fourteen residence halls, the newest of which opened for residence in Spring 2007. On-campus housing can hold more than 7,100 students.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jester_Center" title="Jester Center">Jester Center</a> is the largest residence hall with its capacity of 2,945.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Academic enrollment exceeds the on-campus housing capacity; as a result, most students must live in private residence halls, <a href="/wiki/Housing_cooperative" title="Housing cooperative">housing cooperatives</a>, apartments, or with <a href="/wiki/Fraternities_and_sororities" title="Fraternities and sororities" class="mw-redirect">Greek organizations</a> and other off-campus residences. The Division of Housing and Food Service, which already has the largest market share of 7,000 of the estimated 27,000 beds in the campus area, plans to expand to 9,000 beds in the near future.<sup id="cite_ref-Texan-housing_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Texan-housing-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_organizations">Student organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Student organizations">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The university recognizes more than 1,000 student organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, it supports three official student governance organizations that represent student interests to faculty, administrators, and the Texas Legislature. Student Government, established in 1902, is the oldest governance organization and represents student interests in general.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate of College Councils represents students in academic affairs and coordinates the college councils,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Graduate Student Assembly represents graduate student interests.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> The University Unions Student Events Center serves as the hub for student activities on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Friar_Society" title="Friar Society">Friar Society</a> serves as the oldest honor society at the university.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Texas_4000_for_Cancer" title="Texas 4000 for Cancer">Texas 4000 for Cancer</a> student organization is the longest annual charity bicycle ride in the world and has raised over $1.4 million dollars for cancer research from its founding in 2004 to April, 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_life">Greek life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Greek life">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_fraternities_and_sororities_at_University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="List of fraternities and sororities at University of Texas at Austin">List of fraternities and sororities at University of Texas at Austin</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The University of Texas at Austin is home to an active Greek community. Approximately 14 percent of undergraduate students are in fraternities or sororities.<sup id="cite_ref-Austin_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> With more than 65 national chapters, the university's Greek community is one of the nation's largest.<sup id="cite_ref-Austin_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> These chapters are under the authority of one of the school's six Greek council communities, <a href="/wiki/North-American_Interfraternity_Conference" title="North-American Interfraternity Conference">Interfraternity Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Pan-Hellenic_Council" title="National Pan-Hellenic Council">National Pan-Hellenic Council</a>, Texas Asian Pan-Hellenic Council, Latino Pan-Hellenic Council, Multicultural Greek Council and University Panhellenic Council.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Other registered student organizations also name themselves with Greek letters and are called affiliates. They are not a part of one of the six councils but have all of the same privileges and responsibilities of any other organization.<sup id="cite_ref-GreekGuide_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreekGuide-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the Office of the Dean of Students' mission statement, Greek life promotes cultural appreciation, scholarship, leadership, and <a href="/wiki/Community_service" title="Community service">service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Most Greek houses are west of the <a href="/wiki/Drag_(Austin,_Texas)" title="Drag (Austin, Texas)">Drag</a> in the <a href="/wiki/West_Campus,_Austin,_Texas" title="West Campus, Austin, Texas">West Campus</a> neighborhood.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Texas_Student_Media" title="Texas Student Media">Texas Student Media</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Students express their opinions in and out of class through periodicals including <i><a href="/wiki/Study_Breaks_Magazine" title="Study Breaks Magazine" class="mw-redirect">Study Breaks Magazine</a></i>, Longhorn Life, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Texan" title="The Daily Texan">The Daily Texan</a></i> (the most award-winning daily college newspaper in the United States),<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Texas_Travesty" title="Texas Travesty">Texas Travesty</a></i>. Over the airwaves students' voices are heard through <a href="/wiki/K29HW-D" title="K29HW-D">K29HW-D</a> and <a href="/wiki/KVRX" title="KVRX">KVRX</a>.</p> 
     <p>The Computer Writing and Research Lab of the university's Department of Rhetoric and Writing also hosts <i>the Blogora</i>, a blog for &quot;connecting rhetoric, rhetorical methods and theories, and rhetoricians with public life&quot; by the <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric_Society_of_America" title="Rhetoric Society of America">Rhetoric Society of America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university has a yearbook. In the 1980s it annually sold 14,000 copies. In 1997 it sold 1,700, an all-time low. Kathy Lawrence, the media adviser at UT Austin, said that yearbook sales declined once the school switched from in-person registration to telephone-based registration. During in-person registrations, the university often asked students to buy student yearbooks. Lawrence said that other factors leading to a decline in yearbook sales at UT Austin included increasing student numbers and declining participation in campus life. As of 2008, about 2,500 copies sell annually. To salvage the yearbook, Lawrence introduced personalized pages. When Lawrence concluded that <a href="/wiki/Social_networking_sites" title="Social networking sites" class="mw-redirect">social networking sites</a> lead to a decline in yearbook sales, Sarah Viren of the <i><a href="/wiki/Houston_Chronicle" title="Houston Chronicle">Houston Chronicle</a></i> said that Lawrence &quot;eventually opted to hold off on the personalized pages.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditions">Traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Traditions">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Texas_Longhorn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Texas_Longhorn.jpg/220px-Texas_Longhorn.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Texas_Longhorn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Texas_Longhorn.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Texas_Longhorn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Texas_Longhorn_(cattle)" title="Texas Longhorn (cattle)" class="mw-redirect">Texas longhorn</a> is the university's 
        <a href="/wiki/Mascot" title="Mascot">mascot</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Traditions at the University of Texas are perpetuated through several school symbols and mediums. At athletic events, students frequently sing &quot;<a href="/wiki/Texas_Fight" title="Texas Fight">Texas Fight</a>&quot;, the university's fight song while displaying the <a href="/wiki/Hook_%27em_Horns" title="Hook 'em Horns">Hook 'em Horns</a> hand gesture?the gesture mimicking the horns of the school's mascot, <a href="/wiki/Bevo_(mascot)" title="Bevo (mascot)">Bevo</a> the <a href="/wiki/Texas_longhorn_(cattle)" title="Texas longhorn (cattle)" class="mw-redirect">Texas longhorn</a>.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics">Athletics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Athletics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Texas_Longhorns" title="Texas Longhorns">Texas Longhorns</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Texas_Longhorn_logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a8/Texas_Longhorn_logo.svg/220px-Texas_Longhorn_logo.svg.png" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a8/Texas_Longhorn_logo.svg/330px-Texas_Longhorn_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a8/Texas_Longhorn_logo.svg/440px-Texas_Longhorn_logo.svg.png 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Texas_Longhorn_logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Texas Longhorns logo
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The University of Texas offers a wide variety of varsity and intramural sports programs. As of 2008, the university's athletics program ranked fifth in the nation among <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">Division I</a> schools according to the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Collegiate_Directors_of_Athletics" title="National Association of Collegiate Directors of Athletics">National Association of Collegiate Directors of Athletics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Due to the breadth of sports offered and the quality of the programs, Texas was selected as &quot;America's Best Sports College&quot; in a 2002 analysis by <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Texas was also listed as the number one <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_Licensing_Company" title="Collegiate Licensing Company" class="mw-redirect">Collegiate Licensing Company</a> client for the second consecutive year in regards to the amount of annual <a href="/wiki/Royalties#Trademark_royalties" title="Royalties">trademark royalties</a> received from fan merchandise sales. But this ranking is based only on clients of the Collegiate Licensing Company, which does not handle licensing for approximately three-dozen large schools including <a href="/wiki/Ohio_State_University" title="Ohio State University">Ohio State</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southern_California" title="University of Southern California">USC</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles" title="University of California, Los Angeles">UCLA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan_State_University" title="Michigan State University">Michigan State</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University" title="Texas A&amp;M University">Texas A&amp;M</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Varsity_sports">Varsity sports</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Texas_at_Austin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Varsity sports">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The University's men's and women's athletics teams are nicknamed the Longhorns. A charter member of the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Conference" title="Southwest Conference">Southwest Conference</a> until it dissolved in 1996, Texas now competes in the <a href="/wiki/Big_12_Conference" title="Big 12 Conference">Big 12 Conference</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">NCAA's Division I-FBS</a>. Texas has won 50 total national championships,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> 42 of which are NCAA national championships.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University of Texas has traditionally been considered a <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">college football</a> powerhouse.<sup id="cite_ref-Potomac_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Potomac-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarheel_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarheel-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BC.24_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BC.24-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> At the start of the 2007 season, the Longhorns were ranked third in the all-time list of both total wins and winning percentage.<sup id="cite_ref-Ark_State_gw_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ark_State_gw-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> The team experienced its greatest success under coach <a href="/wiki/Darrell_Royal" title="Darrell Royal">Darrell Royal</a>, winning three national championships in 1963, 1969, and 1970. It won a fourth title under head coach <a href="/wiki/Mack_Brown" title="Mack Brown">Mack Brown</a> in 2005 after a 41?38 victory over previously undefeated <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southern_California" title="University of Southern California">Southern California</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2006_Rose_Bowl" title="2006 Rose Bowl">2006 Rose Bowl</a>.</p> 
     <p>In recent years, the men's <a href="/wiki/College_basketball" title="College basketball">basketball</a> team has gained prominence, advancing to the <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Division I Basketball Championship">NCAA Tournament</a> Sweet Sixteen in 2002, the Final Four in 2003, the Sweet Sixteen in 2004, and the <a href="/wiki/Elite_Eight" title="Elite Eight">Elite Eight</a> in 2006 and 2008.</p> 
     <p>The University's <a href="/wiki/College_baseball" title="College baseball">baseball</a> team is one of the nation's best. It has made more trips to the <a href="/wiki/College_World_Series" title="College World Series">College World Series</a> (34) than any other school, and it posted wins in 1949, 1950, 1975, 1983, 2002, and 2005.</p> 
     <p>Additionally, the University's highly successful men's and women's swimming and diving teams lay claim to sixteen NCAA Division I titles.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> The swim team was first developed under Coach <a href="/wiki/Tex_Robertson" title="Tex Robertson">Tex Robertson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tc_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tc-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> In particular, the men's team is led by <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Reese" title="Eddie Reese">Eddie Reese</a>, who served as the head men's coach at the <a href="/wiki/1992_Summer_Olympics" title="1992 Summer Olympics">1992 Summer Olympics</a> in Barcelona, the <a href="/wiki/2004_Summer_Olympics" title="2004 Summer Olympics">2004 Games</a> in Athens and the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Games</a> in Beijing.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Texas_entry_2007_Red_River_Shootout.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Texas_entry_2007_Red_River_Shootout.jpg/220px-Texas_entry_2007_Red_River_Shootout.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Texas_entry_2007_Red_River_Shootout.jpg/330px-Texas_entry_2007_Red_River_Shootout.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Texas_entry_2007_Red_River_Shootout.jpg/440px-Texas_entry_2007_Red_River_Shootout.jpg 2x" /></a>

        </div> Texas entry in the 2007 
        <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Shootout" title="Red River Shootout" class="mw-redirect">Red River Shootout</a>
       </div>
